    </div>
  </div>
</div>
</div></div><div class="sqs-block html-block sqs-block-html" data-block-type="2" id="block-yui_3_17_2_1_1559830572727_43613"><div class="sqs-block-content">

<div class="sqs-html-content">
  <h3 style="text-align:center;white-space:pre-wrap;">Poetry</h3>
</div>






















</div></div><div class="sqs-block summary-v2-block sqs-block-summary-v2" data-block-json="&#123;&quot;hSize&quot;:null,&quot;floatDir&quot;:null,&quot;collectionId&quot;:&quot;5cf91f2f90a475000140e95b&quot;,&quot;design&quot;:&quot;list&quot;,&quot;headerText&quot;:&quot;Featured&quot;,&quot;textSize&quot;:&quot;small&quot;,&quot;pageSize&quot;:30,&quot;imageAspectRatio&quot;:&quot;1.5&quot;,&quot;columnWidth&quot;:270,&quot;gutter&quot;:60,&quot;listImageSize&quot;:30,&quot;listImageAlignment&quot;:&quot;left&quot;,&quot;slidesPerRow&quot;:3,&quot;textAlignment&quot;:&quot;left&quot;,&quot;showTitle&quot;:true,&quot;showThumbnail&quot;:false,&quot;showExcerpt&quot;:false,&quot;showReadMoreLink&quot;:false,&quot;showPrice&quot;:true,&quot;productQuickViewEnabled&quot;:false,&quot;showPastOrUpcomingEvents&quot;:&quot;upcoming&quot;,&quot;metadataPosition&quot;:&quot;below-content&quot;,&quot;primaryMetadata&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;secondaryMetadata&quot;:&quot;none&quot;,&quot;filter&quot;:&#123;&#125;,&quot;autoCrop&quot;:true,&quot;lightbox&quot;:false,&quot;mixedContent&quot;:true,&quot;blockId&quot;:&quot;1dbb330e9d40d1d37364&quot;&#125;" data-block-type="55" id="block-yui_3_17_2_1_1559830572727_44102"><div class="sqs-block-content">

      <main id="page" class="site-page loading responsive-padding--left responsive-padding--right site-page--blog-item" role="main" data-content-field="main-content" data-controller="FadeInContent">

        <div class="blog-wrapper" data-content-field="main-content" data-item-id="5cf7c9e36c9de600015ed837">
  
  <div class="blog-item-title blog-item-title--top">
    <time class="dt-published date-callout" datetime="2019-06-23" pubdate>
      <div class="date-wrapper">
        <span class="month">Jun </span><span class="day">23</span>
      </div>
    </time>
    <h2 class="entry-title entry-title--large p-name" data-content-field="title"><time class="dt-published date-highlight" datetime="2019-06-23" pubdate>Jun 23 </time>Romeo and Juliet</h2>

    <div class="meta-wrapper">
      <div class="blog-meta-item blog-meta-author"><a href="/tragedy?author=5cf7f8d4c883aa000191747e" class="blog-author-name">William Shakespeare</a> </div><!--

      -->
    </div>
  </div>
  

  <div class="main-content blog-view-item clearfix">
    <input id="show-comments" type="checkbox" />
    <article id="article-5cf7c9e36c9de600015ed837" class="entry--item h-entry clearfix hentry author-william-shakespeare post-type-text">
      
      
      <div class="blog-item-content-wrapper" data-controller="BlogProgressBar">
        
        <div class="pie-wrapper" id="pieWrapper">
          <div class="pie pie--left" id="pieLeft"></div>
          <div class="pie pie--right hide" id="pieRight"></div>
          <div class="mask mask--right hide" id="pieMask"></div>
          <div class="mask mask--left" id="maskLeft"></div>
        </div>
        <div class="blog-item-content e-content"><div class="sqs-layout sqs-grid-12 columns-12" data-layout-label="Post Body" data-type="item" data-updated-on="1559742984172" id="item-5cf7c9e36c9de600015ed837"><div class="row sqs-row"><div class="col sqs-col-12 span-12"><div class="sqs-block html-block sqs-block-html" data-block-type="2" data-border-radii="&#123;&quot;topLeft&quot;:&#123;&quot;unit&quot;:&quot;px&quot;,&quot;value&quot;:0.0&#125;,&quot;topRight&quot;:&#123;&quot;unit&quot;:&quot;px&quot;,&quot;value&quot;:0.0&#125;,&quot;bottomLeft&quot;:&#123;&quot;unit&quot;:&quot;px&quot;,&quot;value&quot;:0.0&#125;,&quot;bottomRight&quot;:&#123;&quot;unit&quot;:&quot;px&quot;,&quot;value&quot;:0.0&#125;&#125;" id="block-4f3c7f34842686d469ff"><div class="sqs-block-content">

<div class="sqs-html-content">
  <h2 style="text-align:center;white-space:pre-wrap;">the full text<br>by William Shakespeare</h2><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">ACT I</h3><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">PROLOGUE</h3><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Two households, both alike in dignity,<br>In fair Verona, where we lay our scene,<br>From ancient grudge break to new mutiny,<br>Where civil blood makes civil hands unclean.<br>From forth the fatal loins of these two foes<br>A pair of star-cross'd lovers take their life;<br>Whose misadventured piteous overthrows<br>Do with their death bury their parents' strife.<br>The fearful passage of their death-mark'd love,<br>And the continuance of their parents' rage,<br>Which, but their children's end, nought could remove,<br>Is now the two hours' traffic of our stage;<br>The which if you with patient ears attend,<br>What here shall miss, our toil shall strive to mend.<br></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE I. Verona. A public place.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter SAMPSON and GREGORY, of the house of Capulet, armed with swords and bucklers</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Gregory, o' my word, we'll not carry coals.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No, for then we should be colliers.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I mean, an we be in choler, we'll draw.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, while you live, draw your neck out o' the collar.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I strike quickly, being moved.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But thou art not quickly moved to strike.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A dog of the house of Montague moves me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">To move is to stir; and to be valiant is to stand:<br>therefore, if thou art moved, thou runn'st away.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A dog of that house shall move me to stand: I will<br>take the wall of any man or maid of Montague's.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">That shows thee a weak slave; for the weakest goes<br>to the wall.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">True; and therefore women, being the weaker vessels,<br>are ever thrust to the wall: therefore I will push<br>Montague's men from the wall, and thrust his maids<br>to the wall.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The quarrel is between our masters and us their men.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis all one, I will show myself a tyrant: when I<br>have fought with the men, I will be cruel with the<br>maids, and cut off their heads.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The heads of the maids?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, the heads of the maids, or their maidenheads;<br>take it in what sense thou wilt.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">They must take it in sense that feel it.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Me they shall feel while I am able to stand: and<br>'tis known I am a pretty piece of flesh.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis well thou art not fish; if thou hadst, thou<br>hadst been poor John. Draw thy tool! here comes<br>two of the house of the Montagues.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My naked weapon is out: quarrel, I will back thee.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">How! turn thy back and run?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Fear me not.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No, marry; I fear thee!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Let us take the law of our sides; let them begin.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will frown as I pass by, and let them take it as<br>they list.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, as they dare. I will bite my thumb at them;<br>which is a disgrace to them, if they bear it.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ABRAHAM and BALTHASAR</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ABRAHAM</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I do bite my thumb, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ABRAHAM</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[Aside to GREGORY] Is the law of our side, if I say<br>ay?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No, sir, I do not bite my thumb at you, sir, but I<br>bite my thumb, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Do you quarrel, sir?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ABRAHAM</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Quarrel sir! no, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">If you do, sir, I am for you: I serve as good a man as you.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ABRAHAM</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No better.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>GREGORY</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Say 'better:' here comes one of my master's kinsmen.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Yes, better, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ABRAHAM</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">You lie.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>SAMPSON</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Draw, if you be men. Gregory, remember thy swashing blow.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>They fight</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter BENVOLIO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Part, fools!<br>Put up your swords; you know not what you do.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Beats down their swords</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter TYBALT</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What, art thou drawn among these heartless hinds?<br>Turn thee, Benvolio, look upon thy death.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I do but keep the peace: put up thy sword,<br>Or manage it to part these men with me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What, drawn, and talk of peace! I hate the word,<br>As I hate hell, all Montagues, and thee:<br>Have at thee, coward!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>They fight</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter, several of both houses, who join the fray; then enter Citizens, with clubs</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Citizen</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Clubs, bills, and partisans! strike! beat them down!<br>Down with the Capulets! down with the Montagues!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter CAPULET in his gown, and LADY CAPULET</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What noise is this? Give me my long sword, ho!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A crutch, a crutch! why call you for a sword?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My sword, I say! Old Montague is come,<br>And flourishes his blade in spite of me.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter MONTAGUE and LADY MONTAGUE</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou villain Capulet,--Hold me not, let me go.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou shalt not stir a foot to seek a foe.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter PRINCE, with Attendants</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Rebellious subjects, enemies to peace,<br>Profaners of this neighbour-stained steel,--<br>Will they not hear? What, ho! you men, you beasts,<br>That quench the fire of your pernicious rage<br>With purple fountains issuing from your veins,<br>On pain of torture, from those bloody hands<br>Throw your mistemper'd weapons to the ground,<br>And hear the sentence of your moved prince.<br>Three civil brawls, bred of an airy word,<br>By thee, old Capulet, and Montague,<br>Have thrice disturb'd the quiet of our streets,<br>And made Verona's ancient citizens<br>Cast by their grave beseeming ornaments,<br>To wield old partisans, in hands as old,<br>Canker'd with peace, to part your canker'd hate:<br>If ever you disturb our streets again,<br>Your lives shall pay the forfeit of the peace.<br>For this time, all the rest depart away:<br>You Capulet; shall go along with me:<br>And, Montague, come you this afternoon,<br>To know our further pleasure in this case,<br>To old Free-town, our common judgment-place.<br>Once more, on pain of death, all men depart.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt all but MONTAGUE, LADY MONTAGUE, and BENVOLIO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Who set this ancient quarrel new abroach?<br>Speak, nephew, were you by when it began?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here were the servants of your adversary,<br>And yours, close fighting ere I did approach:<br>I drew to part them: in the instant came<br>The fiery Tybalt, with his sword prepared,<br>Which, as he breathed defiance to my ears,<br>He swung about his head and cut the winds,<br>Who nothing hurt withal hiss'd him in scorn:<br>While we were interchanging thrusts and blows,<br>Came more and more and fought on part and part,<br>Till the prince came, who parted either part.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, where is Romeo? saw you him to-day?<br>Right glad I am he was not at this fray.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Madam, an hour before the worshipp'd sun<br>Peer'd forth the golden window of the east,<br>A troubled mind drave me to walk abroad;<br>Where, underneath the grove of sycamore<br>That westward rooteth from the city's side,<br>So early walking did I see your son:<br>Towards him I made, but he was ware of me<br>And stole into the covert of the wood:<br>I, measuring his affections by my own,<br>That most are busied when they're most alone,<br>Pursued my humour not pursuing his,<br>And gladly shunn'd who gladly fled from me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Many a morning hath he there been seen,<br>With tears augmenting the fresh morning dew.<br>Adding to clouds more clouds with his deep sighs;<br>But all so soon as the all-cheering sun<br>Should in the furthest east begin to draw<br>The shady curtains from Aurora's bed,<br>Away from the light steals home my heavy son,<br>And private in his chamber pens himself,<br>Shuts up his windows, locks far daylight out<br>And makes himself an artificial night:<br>Black and portentous must this humour prove,<br>Unless good counsel may the cause remove.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My noble uncle, do you know the cause?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I neither know it nor can learn of him.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Have you importuned him by any means?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Both by myself and many other friends:<br>But he, his own affections' counsellor,<br>Is to himself--I will not say how true--<br>But to himself so secret and so close,<br>So far from sounding and discovery,<br>As is the bud bit with an envious worm,<br>Ere he can spread his sweet leaves to the air,<br>Or dedicate his beauty to the sun.<br>Could we but learn from whence his sorrows grow.<br>We would as willingly give cure as know.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">See, where he comes: so please you, step aside;<br>I'll know his grievance, or be much denied.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I would thou wert so happy by thy stay,<br>To hear true shrift. Come, madam, let's away.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt MONTAGUE and LADY MONTAGUE</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good-morrow, cousin.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Is the day so young?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But new struck nine.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay me! sad hours seem long.<br>Was that my father that went hence so fast?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">It was. What sadness lengthens Romeo's hours?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Not having that, which, having, makes them short.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">In love?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Out--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Of love?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Out of her favour, where I am in love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Alas, that love, so gentle in his view,<br>Should be so tyrannous and rough in proof!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Alas, that love, whose view is muffled still,<br>Should, without eyes, see pathways to his will!<br>Where shall we dine? O me! What fray was here?<br>Yet tell me not, for I have heard it all.<br>Here's much to do with hate, but more with love.<br>Why, then, O brawling love! O loving hate!<br>O any thing, of nothing first create!<br>O heavy lightness! serious vanity!<br>Mis-shapen chaos of well-seeming forms!<br>Feather of lead, bright smoke, cold fire,<br>sick health!<br>Still-waking sleep, that is not what it is!<br>This love feel I, that feel no love in this.<br>Dost thou not laugh?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No, coz, I rather weep.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good heart, at what?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">At thy good heart's oppression.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, such is love's transgression.<br>Griefs of mine own lie heavy in my breast,<br>Which thou wilt propagate, to have it prest<br>With more of thine: this love that thou hast shown<br>Doth add more grief to too much of mine own.<br>Love is a smoke raised with the fume of sighs;<br>Being purged, a fire sparkling in lovers' eyes;<br>Being vex'd a sea nourish'd with lovers' tears:<br>What is it else? a madness most discreet,<br>A choking gall and a preserving sweet.<br>Farewell, my coz.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Soft! I will go along;<br>An if you leave me so, you do me wrong.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tut, I have lost myself; I am not here;<br>This is not Romeo, he's some other where.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tell me in sadness, who is that you love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What, shall I groan and tell thee?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Groan! why, no.<br>But sadly tell me who.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Bid a sick man in sadness make his will:<br>Ah, word ill urged to one that is so ill!<br>In sadness, cousin, I do love a woman.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I aim'd so near, when I supposed you loved.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A right good mark-man! And she's fair I love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A right fair mark, fair coz, is soonest hit.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, in that hit you miss: she'll not be hit<br>With Cupid's arrow; she hath Dian's wit;<br>And, in strong proof of chastity well arm'd,<br>From love's weak childish bow she lives unharm'd.<br>She will not stay the siege of loving terms,<br>Nor bide the encounter of assailing eyes,<br>Nor ope her lap to saint-seducing gold:<br>O, she is rich in beauty, only poor,<br>That when she dies with beauty dies her store.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then she hath sworn that she will still live chaste?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">She hath, and in that sparing makes huge waste,<br>For beauty starved with her severity<br>Cuts beauty off from all posterity.<br>She is too fair, too wise, wisely too fair,<br>To merit bliss by making me despair:<br>She hath forsworn to love, and in that vow<br>Do I live dead that live to tell it now.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Be ruled by me, forget to think of her.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, teach me how I should forget to think.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By giving liberty unto thine eyes;<br>Examine other beauties.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis the way<br>To call hers exquisite, in question more:<br>These happy masks that kiss fair ladies' brows<br>Being black put us in mind they hide the fair;<br>He that is strucken blind cannot forget<br>The precious treasure of his eyesight lost:<br>Show me a mistress that is passing fair,<br>What doth her beauty serve, but as a note<br>Where I may read who pass'd that passing fair?<br>Farewell: thou canst not teach me to forget.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I'll pay that doctrine, or else die in debt.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE II. A street.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter CAPULET, PARIS, and Servant</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But Montague is bound as well as I,<br>In penalty alike; and 'tis not hard, I think,<br>For men so old as we to keep the peace.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PARIS</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Of honourable reckoning are you both;<br>And pity 'tis you lived at odds so long.<br>But now, my lord, what say you to my suit?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But saying o'er what I have said before:<br>My child is yet a stranger in the world;<br>She hath not seen the change of fourteen years,<br>Let two more summers wither in their pride,<br>Ere we may think her ripe to be a bride.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PARIS</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Younger than she are happy mothers made.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And too soon marr'd are those so early made.<br>The earth hath swallow'd all my hopes but she,<br>She is the hopeful lady of my earth:<br>But woo her, gentle Paris, get her heart,<br>My will to her consent is but a part;<br>An she agree, within her scope of choice<br>Lies my consent and fair according voice.<br>This night I hold an old accustom'd feast,<br>Whereto I have invited many a guest,<br>Such as I love; and you, among the store,<br>One more, most welcome, makes my number more.<br>At my poor house look to behold this night<br>Earth-treading stars that make dark heaven light:<br>Such comfort as do lusty young men feel<br>When well-apparell'd April on the heel<br>Of limping winter treads, even such delight<br>Among fresh female buds shall you this night<br>Inherit at my house; hear all, all see,<br>And like her most whose merit most shall be:<br>Which on more view, of many mine being one<br>May stand in number, though in reckoning none,<br>Come, go with me.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>To Servant, giving a paper</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go, sirrah, trudge about<br>Through fair Verona; find those persons out<br>Whose names are written there, and to them say,<br>My house and welcome on their pleasure stay.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt CAPULET and PARIS</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Find them out whose names are written here! It is<br>written, that the shoemaker should meddle with his<br>yard, and the tailor with his last, the fisher with<br>his pencil, and the painter with his nets; but I am<br>sent to find those persons whose names are here<br>writ, and can never find what names the writing<br>person hath here writ. I must to the learned.--In good time.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter BENVOLIO and ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tut, man, one fire burns out another's burning,<br>One pain is lessen'd by another's anguish;<br>Turn giddy, and be holp by backward turning;<br>One desperate grief cures with another's languish:<br>Take thou some new infection to thy eye,<br>And the rank poison of the old will die.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Your plaintain-leaf is excellent for that.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">For what, I pray thee?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">For your broken shin.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, Romeo, art thou mad?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Not mad, but bound more than a mad-man is;<br>Shut up in prison, kept without my food,<br>Whipp'd and tormented and--God-den, good fellow.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">God gi' god-den. I pray, sir, can you read?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, mine own fortune in my misery.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Perhaps you have learned it without book: but, I<br>pray, can you read any thing you see?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, if I know the letters and the language.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ye say honestly: rest you merry!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Stay, fellow; I can read.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Reads</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Signior Martino and his wife and daughters;<br>County Anselme and his beauteous sisters; the lady<br>widow of Vitravio; Signior Placentio and his lovely<br>nieces; Mercutio and his brother Valentine; mine<br>uncle Capulet, his wife and daughters; my fair niece<br>Rosaline; Livia; Signior Valentio and his cousin<br>Tybalt, Lucio and the lively Helena.' A fair<br>assembly: whither should they come?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Up.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Whither?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">To supper; to our house.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Whose house?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My master's.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Indeed, I should have ask'd you that before.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now I'll tell you without asking: my master is the<br>great rich Capulet; and if you be not of the house<br>of Montagues, I pray, come and crush a cup of wine.<br>Rest you merry!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">At this same ancient feast of Capulet's<br>Sups the fair Rosaline whom thou so lovest,<br>With all the admired beauties of Verona:<br>Go thither; and, with unattainted eye,<br>Compare her face with some that I shall show,<br>And I will make thee think thy swan a crow.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">When the devout religion of mine eye<br>Maintains such falsehood, then turn tears to fires;<br>And these, who often drown'd could never die,<br>Transparent heretics, be burnt for liars!<br>One fairer than my love! the all-seeing sun<br>Ne'er saw her match since first the world begun.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tut, you saw her fair, none else being by,<br>Herself poised with herself in either eye:<br>But in that crystal scales let there be weigh'd<br>Your lady's love against some other maid<br>That I will show you shining at this feast,<br>And she shall scant show well that now shows best.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I'll go along, no such sight to be shown,<br>But to rejoice in splendor of mine own.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE III. A room in Capulet's house.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter LADY CAPULET and Nurse</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nurse, where's my daughter? call her forth to me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now, by my maidenhead, at twelve year old,<br>I bade her come. What, lamb! what, ladybird!<br>God forbid! Where's this girl? What, Juliet!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter JULIET</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">How now! who calls?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Your mother.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Madam, I am here.<br>What is your will?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This is the matter:--Nurse, give leave awhile,<br>We must talk in secret:--nurse, come back again;<br>I have remember'd me, thou's hear our counsel.<br>Thou know'st my daughter's of a pretty age.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Faith, I can tell her age unto an hour.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">She's not fourteen.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I'll lay fourteen of my teeth,--<br>And yet, to my teeth be it spoken, I have but four--<br>She is not fourteen. How long is it now<br>To Lammas-tide?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A fortnight and odd days.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Even or odd, of all days in the year,<br>Come Lammas-eve at night shall she be fourteen.<br>Susan and she--God rest all Christian souls!--<br>Were of an age: well, Susan is with God;<br>She was too good for me: but, as I said,<br>On Lammas-eve at night shall she be fourteen;<br>That shall she, marry; I remember it well.<br>'Tis since the earthquake now eleven years;<br>And she was wean'd,--I never shall forget it,--<br>Of all the days of the year, upon that day:<br>For I had then laid wormwood to my dug,<br>Sitting in the sun under the dove-house wall;<br>My lord and you were then at Mantua:--<br>Nay, I do bear a brain:--but, as I said,<br>When it did taste the wormwood on the nipple<br>Of my dug and felt it bitter, pretty fool,<br>To see it tetchy and fall out with the dug!<br>Shake quoth the dove-house: 'twas no need, I trow,<br>To bid me trudge:<br>And since that time it is eleven years;<br>For then she could stand alone; nay, by the rood,<br>She could have run and waddled all about;<br>For even the day before, she broke her brow:<br>And then my husband--God be with his soul!<br>A' was a merry man--took up the child:<br>'Yea,' quoth he, 'dost thou fall upon thy face?<br>Thou wilt fall backward when thou hast more wit;<br>Wilt thou not, Jule?' and, by my holidame,<br>The pretty wretch left crying and said 'Ay.'<br>To see, now, how a jest shall come about!<br>I warrant, an I should live a thousand years,<br>I never should forget it: 'Wilt thou not, Jule?' quoth he;<br>And, pretty fool, it stinted and said 'Ay.'<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Enough of this; I pray thee, hold thy peace.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Yes, madam: yet I cannot choose but laugh,<br>To think it should leave crying and say 'Ay.'<br>And yet, I warrant, it had upon its brow<br>A bump as big as a young cockerel's stone;<br>A parlous knock; and it cried bitterly:<br>'Yea,' quoth my husband,'fall'st upon thy face?<br>Thou wilt fall backward when thou comest to age;<br>Wilt thou not, Jule?' it stinted and said 'Ay.'<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And stint thou too, I pray thee, nurse, say I.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Peace, I have done. God mark thee to his grace!<br>Thou wast the prettiest babe that e'er I nursed:<br>An I might live to see thee married once,<br>I have my wish.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Marry, that 'marry' is the very theme<br>I came to talk of. Tell me, daughter Juliet,<br>How stands your disposition to be married?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">It is an honour that I dream not of.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">An honour! were not I thine only nurse,<br>I would say thou hadst suck'd wisdom from thy teat.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, think of marriage now; younger than you,<br>Here in Verona, ladies of esteem,<br>Are made already mothers: by my count,<br>I was your mother much upon these years<br>That you are now a maid. Thus then in brief:<br>The valiant Paris seeks you for his love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A man, young lady! lady, such a man<br>As all the world--why, he's a man of wax.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Verona's summer hath not such a flower.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, he's a flower; in faith, a very flower.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What say you? can you love the gentleman?<br>This night you shall behold him at our feast;<br>Read o'er the volume of young Paris' face,<br>And find delight writ there with beauty's pen;<br>Examine every married lineament,<br>And see how one another lends content<br>And what obscured in this fair volume lies<br>Find written in the margent of his eyes.<br>This precious book of love, this unbound lover,<br>To beautify him, only lacks a cover:<br>The fish lives in the sea, and 'tis much pride<br>For fair without the fair within to hide:<br>That book in many's eyes doth share the glory,<br>That in gold clasps locks in the golden story;<br>So shall you share all that he doth possess,<br>By having him, making yourself no less.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No less! nay, bigger; women grow by men.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Speak briefly, can you like of Paris' love?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I'll look to like, if looking liking move:<br>But no more deep will I endart mine eye<br>Than your consent gives strength to make it fly.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter a Servant</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Madam, the guests are come, supper served up, you<br>called, my young lady asked for, the nurse cursed in<br>the pantry, and every thing in extremity. I must<br>hence to wait; I beseech you, follow straight.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">We follow thee.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit Servant</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Juliet, the county stays.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go, girl, seek happy nights to happy days.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE IV. A street.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO, MERCUTIO, BENVOLIO, with five or six Maskers, Torch-bearers, and others</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What, shall this speech be spoke for our excuse?<br>Or shall we on without a apology?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The date is out of such prolixity:<br>We'll have no Cupid hoodwink'd with a scarf,<br>Bearing a Tartar's painted bow of lath,<br>Scaring the ladies like a crow-keeper;<br>Nor no without-book prologue, faintly spoke<br>After the prompter, for our entrance:<br>But let them measure us by what they will;<br>We'll measure them a measure, and be gone.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Give me a torch: I am not for this ambling;<br>Being but heavy, I will bear the light.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, gentle Romeo, we must have you dance.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Not I, believe me: you have dancing shoes<br>With nimble soles: I have a soul of lead<br>So stakes me to the ground I cannot move.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">You are a lover; borrow Cupid's wings,<br>And soar with them above a common bound.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I am too sore enpierced with his shaft<br>To soar with his light feathers, and so bound,<br>I cannot bound a pitch above dull woe:<br>Under love's heavy burden do I sink.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And, to sink in it, should you burden love;<br>Too great oppression for a tender thing.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Is love a tender thing? it is too rough,<br>Too rude, too boisterous, and it pricks like thorn.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">If love be rough with you, be rough with love;<br>Prick love for pricking, and you beat love down.<br>Give me a case to put my visage in:<br>A visor for a visor! what care I<br>What curious eye doth quote deformities?<br>Here are the beetle brows shall blush for me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come, knock and enter; and no sooner in,<br>But every man betake him to his legs.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A torch for me: let wantons light of heart<br>Tickle the senseless rushes with their heels,<br>For I am proverb'd with a grandsire phrase;<br>I'll be a candle-holder, and look on.<br>The game was ne'er so fair, and I am done.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tut, dun's the mouse, the constable's own word:<br>If thou art dun, we'll draw thee from the mire<br>Of this sir-reverence love, wherein thou stick'st<br>Up to the ears. Come, we burn daylight, ho!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, that's not so.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I mean, sir, in delay<br>We waste our lights in vain, like lamps by day.<br>Take our good meaning, for our judgment sits<br>Five times in that ere once in our five wits.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And we mean well in going to this mask;<br>But 'tis no wit to go.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, may one ask?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I dream'd a dream to-night.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And so did I.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, what was yours?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">That dreamers often lie.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">In bed asleep, while they do dream things true.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, then, I see Queen Mab hath been with you.<br>She is the fairies' midwife, and she comes<br>In shape no bigger than an agate-stone<br>On the fore-finger of an alderman,<br>Drawn with a team of little atomies<br>Athwart men's noses as they lie asleep;<br>Her wagon-spokes made of long spiders' legs,<br>The cover of the wings of grasshoppers,<br>The traces of the smallest spider's web,<br>The collars of the moonshine's watery beams,<br>Her whip of cricket's bone, the lash of film,<br>Her wagoner a small grey-coated gnat,<br>Not so big as a round little worm<br>Prick'd from the lazy finger of a maid;<br>Her chariot is an empty hazel-nut<br>Made by the joiner squirrel or old grub,<br>Time out o' mind the fairies' coachmakers.<br>And in this state she gallops night by night<br>Through lovers' brains, and then they dream of love;<br>O'er courtiers' knees, that dream on court'sies straight,<br>O'er lawyers' fingers, who straight dream on fees,<br>O'er ladies ' lips, who straight on kisses dream,<br>Which oft the angry Mab with blisters plagues,<br>Because their breaths with sweetmeats tainted are:<br>Sometime she gallops o'er a courtier's nose,<br>And then dreams he of smelling out a suit;<br>And sometime comes she with a tithe-pig's tail<br>Tickling a parson's nose as a' lies asleep,<br>Then dreams, he of another benefice:<br>Sometime she driveth o'er a soldier's neck,<br>And then dreams he of cutting foreign throats,<br>Of breaches, ambuscadoes, Spanish blades,<br>Of healths five-fathom deep; and then anon<br>Drums in his ear, at which he starts and wakes,<br>And being thus frighted swears a prayer or two<br>And sleeps again. This is that very Mab<br>That plats the manes of horses in the night,<br>And bakes the elflocks in foul sluttish hairs,<br>Which once untangled, much misfortune bodes:<br>This is the hag, when maids lie on their backs,<br>That presses them and learns them first to bear,<br>Making them women of good carriage:<br>This is she--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Peace, peace, Mercutio, peace!<br>Thou talk'st of nothing.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">True, I talk of dreams,<br>Which are the children of an idle brain,<br>Begot of nothing but vain fantasy,<br>Which is as thin of substance as the air<br>And more inconstant than the wind, who wooes<br>Even now the frozen bosom of the north,<br>And, being anger'd, puffs away from thence,<br>Turning his face to the dew-dropping south.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This wind, you talk of, blows us from ourselves;<br>Supper is done, and we shall come too late.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I fear, too early: for my mind misgives<br>Some consequence yet hanging in the stars<br>Shall bitterly begin his fearful date<br>With this night's revels and expire the term<br>Of a despised life closed in my breast<br>By some vile forfeit of untimely death.<br>But He, that hath the steerage of my course,<br>Direct my sail! On, lusty gentlemen.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Strike, drum.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE V. A hall in Capulet's house.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Musicians waiting. Enter Servingmen with napkins</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Where's Potpan, that he helps not to take away? He<br>shift a trencher? he scrape a trencher!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">When good manners shall lie all in one or two men's<br>hands and they unwashed too, 'tis a foul thing.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Away with the joint-stools, remove the<br>court-cupboard, look to the plate. Good thou, save<br>me a piece of marchpane; and, as thou lovest me, let<br>the porter let in Susan Grindstone and Nell.<br>Antony, and Potpan!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, boy, ready.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">You are looked for and called for, asked for and<br>sought for, in the great chamber.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">We cannot be here and there too. Cheerly, boys; be<br>brisk awhile, and the longer liver take all.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter CAPULET, with JULIET and others of his house, meeting the Guests and Maskers</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Welcome, gentlemen! ladies that have their toes<br>Unplagued with corns will have a bout with you.<br>Ah ha, my mistresses! which of you all<br>Will now deny to dance? she that makes dainty,<br>She, I'll swear, hath corns; am I come near ye now?<br>Welcome, gentlemen! I have seen the day<br>That I have worn a visor and could tell<br>A whispering tale in a fair lady's ear,<br>Such as would please: 'tis gone, 'tis gone, 'tis gone:<br>You are welcome, gentlemen! come, musicians, play.<br>A hall, a hall! give room! and foot it, girls.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Music plays, and they dance</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">More light, you knaves; and turn the tables up,<br>And quench the fire, the room is grown too hot.<br>Ah, sirrah, this unlook'd-for sport comes well.<br>Nay, sit, nay, sit, good cousin Capulet;<br>For you and I are past our dancing days:<br>How long is't now since last yourself and I<br>Were in a mask?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Capulet</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By'r lady, thirty years.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What, man! 'tis not so much, 'tis not so much:<br>'Tis since the nuptials of Lucentio,<br>Come pentecost as quickly as it will,<br>Some five and twenty years; and then we mask'd.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Capulet</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis more, 'tis more, his son is elder, sir;<br>His son is thirty.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Will you tell me that?<br>His son was but a ward two years ago.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[To a Servingman] What lady is that, which doth<br>enrich the hand<br>Of yonder knight?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Servant</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I know not, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, she doth teach the torches to burn bright!<br>It seems she hangs upon the cheek of night<br>Like a rich jewel in an Ethiope's ear;<br>Beauty too rich for use, for earth too dear!<br>So shows a snowy dove trooping with crows,<br>As yonder lady o'er her fellows shows.<br>The measure done, I'll watch her place of stand,<br>And, touching hers, make blessed my rude hand.<br>Did my heart love till now? forswear it, sight!<br>For I ne'er saw true beauty till this night.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This, by his voice, should be a Montague.<br>Fetch me my rapier, boy. What dares the slave<br>Come hither, cover'd with an antic face,<br>To fleer and scorn at our solemnity?<br>Now, by the stock and honour of my kin,<br>To strike him dead, I hold it not a sin.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, how now, kinsman! wherefore storm you so?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Uncle, this is a Montague, our foe,<br>A villain that is hither come in spite,<br>To scorn at our solemnity this night.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Young Romeo is it?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis he, that villain Romeo.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Content thee, gentle coz, let him alone;<br>He bears him like a portly gentleman;<br>And, to say truth, Verona brags of him<br>To be a virtuous and well-govern'd youth:<br>I would not for the wealth of all the town<br>Here in my house do him disparagement:<br>Therefore be patient, take no note of him:<br>It is my will, the which if thou respect,<br>Show a fair presence and put off these frowns,<br>And ill-beseeming semblance for a feast.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">It fits, when such a villain is a guest:<br>I'll not endure him.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">He shall be endured:<br>What, goodman boy! I say, he shall: go to;<br>Am I the master here, or you? go to.<br>You'll not endure him! God shall mend my soul!<br>You'll make a mutiny among my guests!<br>You will set cock-a-hoop! you'll be the man!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, uncle, 'tis a shame.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go to, go to;<br>You are a saucy boy: is't so, indeed?<br>This trick may chance to scathe you, I know what:<br>You must contrary me! marry, 'tis time.<br>Well said, my hearts! You are a princox; go:<br>Be quiet, or--More light, more light! For shame!<br>I'll make you quiet. What, cheerly, my hearts!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Patience perforce with wilful choler meeting<br>Makes my flesh tremble in their different greeting.<br>I will withdraw: but this intrusion shall<br>Now seeming sweet convert to bitter gall.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[To JULIET] If I profane with my unworthiest hand<br>This holy shrine, the gentle fine is this:<br>My lips, two blushing pilgrims, ready stand<br>To smooth that rough touch with a tender kiss.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good pilgrim, you do wrong your hand too much,<br>Which mannerly devotion shows in this;<br>For saints have hands that pilgrims' hands do touch,<br>And palm to palm is holy palmers' kiss.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Have not saints lips, and holy palmers too?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, pilgrim, lips that they must use in prayer.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, then, dear saint, let lips do what hands do;<br>They pray, grant thou, lest faith turn to despair.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Saints do not move, though grant for prayers' sake.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then move not, while my prayer's effect I take.<br>Thus from my lips, by yours, my sin is purged.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then have my lips the sin that they have took.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Sin from thy lips? O trespass sweetly urged!<br>Give me my sin again.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">You kiss by the book.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Madam, your mother craves a word with you.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What is her mother?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Marry, bachelor,<br>Her mother is the lady of the house,<br>And a good lady, and a wise and virtuous<br>I nursed her daughter, that you talk'd withal;<br>I tell you, he that can lay hold of her<br>Shall have the chinks.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Is she a Capulet?<br>O dear account! my life is my foe's debt.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Away, begone; the sport is at the best.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, so I fear; the more is my unrest.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, gentlemen, prepare not to be gone;<br>We have a trifling foolish banquet towards.<br>Is it e'en so? why, then, I thank you all<br>I thank you, honest gentlemen; good night.<br>More torches here! Come on then, let's to bed.<br>Ah, sirrah, by my fay, it waxes late:<br>I'll to my rest.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt all but JULIET and Nurse</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come hither, nurse. What is yond gentleman?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The son and heir of old Tiberio.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What's he that now is going out of door?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Marry, that, I think, be young Petrucio.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What's he that follows there, that would not dance?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I know not.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go ask his name: if he be married.<br>My grave is like to be my wedding bed.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">His name is Romeo, and a Montague;<br>The only son of your great enemy.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My only love sprung from my only hate!<br>Too early seen unknown, and known too late!<br>Prodigious birth of love it is to me,<br>That I must love a loathed enemy.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What's this? what's this?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A rhyme I learn'd even now<br>Of one I danced withal.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>One calls within 'Juliet.'</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Anon, anon!<br>Come, let's away; the strangers all are gone.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">ACT II</h3><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">PROLOGUE</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Chorus</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Chorus</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now old desire doth in his death-bed lie,<br>And young affection gapes to be his heir;<br>That fair for which love groan'd for and would die,<br>With tender Juliet match'd, is now not fair.<br>Now Romeo is beloved and loves again,<br>Alike betwitched by the charm of looks,<br>But to his foe supposed he must complain,<br>And she steal love's sweet bait from fearful hooks:<br>Being held a foe, he may not have access<br>To breathe such vows as lovers use to swear;<br>And she as much in love, her means much less<br>To meet her new-beloved any where:<br>But passion lends them power, time means, to meet<br>Tempering extremities with extreme sweet.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE I. A lane by the wall of Capulet's orchard.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Can I go forward when my heart is here?<br>Turn back, dull earth, and find thy centre out.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>He climbs the wall, and leaps down within it</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter BENVOLIO and MERCUTIO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo! my cousin Romeo!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">He is wise;<br>And, on my lie, hath stol'n him home to bed.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">He ran this way, and leap'd this orchard wall:<br>Call, good Mercutio.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, I'll conjure too.<br>Romeo! humours! madman! passion! lover!<br>Appear thou in the likeness of a sigh:<br>Speak but one rhyme, and I am satisfied;<br>Cry but 'Ay me!' pronounce but 'love' and 'dove;'<br>Speak to my gossip Venus one fair word,<br>One nick-name for her purblind son and heir,<br>Young Adam Cupid, he that shot so trim,<br>When King Cophetua loved the beggar-maid!<br>He heareth not, he stirreth not, he moveth not;<br>The ape is dead, and I must conjure him.<br>I conjure thee by Rosaline's bright eyes,<br>By her high forehead and her scarlet lip,<br>By her fine foot, straight leg and quivering thigh<br>And the demesnes that there adjacent lie,<br>That in thy likeness thou appear to us!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And if he hear thee, thou wilt anger him.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This cannot anger him: 'twould anger him<br>To raise a spirit in his mistress' circle<br>Of some strange nature, letting it there stand<br>Till she had laid it and conjured it down;<br>That were some spite: my invocation<br>Is fair and honest, and in his mistres s' name<br>I conjure only but to raise up him.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come, he hath hid himself among these trees,<br>To be consorted with the humorous night:<br>Blind is his love and best befits the dark.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">If love be blind, love cannot hit the mark.<br>Now will he sit under a medlar tree,<br>And wish his mistress were that kind of fruit<br>As maids call medlars, when they laugh alone.<br>Romeo, that she were, O, that she were<br>An open et caetera, thou a poperin pear!<br>Romeo, good night: I'll to my truckle-bed;<br>This field-bed is too cold for me to sleep:<br>Come, shall we go?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go, then; for 'tis in vain<br>To seek him here that means not to be found.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE II. Capulet's orchard.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">He jests at scars that never felt a wound.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>JULIET appears above at a window</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But, soft!
