what light through yonder window breaks?<br>It is the east, and Juliet is the sun.<br>Arise, fair sun, and kill the envious moon,<br>Who is already sick and pale with grief,<br>That thou her maid art far more fair than she:<br>Be not her maid, since she is envious;<br>Her vestal livery is but sick and green<br>And none but fools do wear it; cast it off.<br>It is my lady, O, it is my love!<br>O, that she knew she were!<br>She speaks yet she says nothing: what of that?<br>Her eye discourses; I will answer it.<br>I am too bold, 'tis not to me she speaks:<br>Two of the fairest stars in all the heaven,<br>Having some business, do entreat her eyes<br>To twinkle in their spheres till they return.<br>What if her eyes were there, they in her head?<br>The brightness of her cheek would shame those stars,<br>As daylight doth a lamp; her eyes in heaven<br>Would through the airy region stream so bright<br>That birds would sing and think it were not night.<br>See, how she leans her cheek upon her hand!<br>O, that I were a glove upon that hand,<br>That I might touch that cheek!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay me!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">She speaks:<br>O, speak again, bright angel! for thou art<br>As glorious to this night, being o'er my head<br>As is a winged messenger of heaven<br>Unto the white-upturned wondering eyes<br>Of mortals that fall back to gaze on him<br>When he bestrides the lazy-pacing clouds<br>And sails upon the bosom of the air.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O Romeo, Romeo! wherefore art thou Romeo?<br>Deny thy father and refuse thy name;<br>Or, if thou wilt not, be but sworn my love,<br>And I'll no longer be a Capulet.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[Aside] Shall I hear more, or shall I speak at this?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis but thy name that is my enemy;<br>Thou art thyself, though not a Montague.<br>What's Montague? it is nor hand, nor foot,<br>Nor arm, nor face, nor any other part<br>Belonging to a man. O, be some other name!<br>What's in a name? that which we call a rose<br>By any other name would smell as sweet;<br>So Romeo would, were he not Romeo call'd,<br>Retain that dear perfection which he owes<br>Without that title. Romeo, doff thy name,<br>And for that name which is no part of thee<br>Take all myself.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I take thee at thy word:<br>Call me but love, and I'll be new baptized;<br>Henceforth I never will be Romeo.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What man art thou that thus bescreen'd in night<br>So stumblest on my counsel?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By a name<br>I know not how to tell thee who I am:<br>My name, dear saint, is hateful to myself,<br>Because it is an enemy to thee;<br>Had I it written, I would tear the word.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My ears have not yet drunk a hundred words<br>Of that tongue's utterance, yet I know the sound:<br>Art thou not Romeo and a Montague?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Neither, fair saint, if either thee dislike.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">How camest thou hither, tell me, and wherefore?<br>The orchard walls are high and hard to climb,<br>And the place death, considering who thou art,<br>If any of my kinsmen find thee here.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">With love's light wings did I o'er-perch these walls;<br>For stony limits cannot hold love out,<br>And what love can do that dares love attempt;<br>Therefore thy kinsmen are no let to me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">If they do see thee, they will murder thee.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Alack, there lies more peril in thine eye<br>Than twenty of their swords: look thou but sweet,<br>And I am proof against their enmity.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I would not for the world they saw thee here.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I have night's cloak to hide me from their sight;<br>And but thou love me, let them find me here:<br>My life were better ended by their hate,<br>Than death prorogued, wanting of thy love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By whose direction found'st thou out this place?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By love, who first did prompt me to inquire;<br>He lent me counsel and I lent him eyes.<br>I am no pilot; yet, wert thou as far<br>As that vast shore wash'd with the farthest sea,<br>I would adventure for such merchandise.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou know'st the mask of night is on my face,<br>Else would a maiden blush bepaint my cheek<br>For that which thou hast heard me speak to-night<br>Fain would I dwell on form, fain, fain deny<br>What I have spoke: but farewell compliment!<br>Dost thou love me? I know thou wilt say 'Ay,'<br>And I will take thy word: yet if thou swear'st,<br>Thou mayst prove false; at lovers' perjuries<br>Then say, Jove laughs. O gentle Romeo,<br>If thou dost love, pronounce it faithfully:<br>Or if thou think'st I am too quickly won,<br>I'll frown and be perverse an say thee nay,<br>So thou wilt woo; but else, not for the world.<br>In truth, fair Montague, I am too fond,<br>And therefore thou mayst think my 'havior light:<br>But trust me, gentleman, I'll prove more true<br>Than those that have more cunning to be strange.<br>I should have been more strange, I must confess,<br>But that thou overheard'st, ere I was ware,<br>My true love's passion: therefore pardon me,<br>And not impute this yielding to light love,<br>Which the dark night hath so discovered.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Lady, by yonder blessed moon I swear<br>That tips with silver all these fruit-tree tops--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, swear not by the moon, the inconstant moon,<br>That monthly changes in her circled orb,<br>Lest that thy love prove likewise variable.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What shall I swear by?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Do not swear at all;<br>Or, if thou wilt, swear by thy gracious self,<br>Which is the god of my idolatry,<br>And I'll believe thee.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">If my heart's dear love--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, do not swear: although I joy in thee,<br>I have no joy of this contract to-night:<br>It is too rash, too unadvised, too sudden;<br>Too like the lightning, which doth cease to be<br>Ere one can say 'It lightens.' Sweet, good night!<br>This bud of love, by summer's ripening breath,<br>May prove a beauteous flower when next we meet.<br>Good night, good night! as sweet repose and rest<br>Come to thy heart as that within my breast!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, wilt thou leave me so unsatisfied?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What satisfaction canst thou have to-night?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The exchange of thy love's faithful vow for mine.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I gave thee mine before thou didst request it:<br>And yet I would it were to give again.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Wouldst thou withdraw it? for what purpose, love?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But to be frank, and give it thee again.<br>And yet I wish but for the thing I have:<br>My bounty is as boundless as the sea,<br>My love as deep; the more I give to thee,<br>The more I have, for both are infinite.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Nurse calls within</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I hear some noise within; dear love, adieu!<br>Anon, good nurse! Sweet Montague, be true.<br>Stay but a little, I will come again.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit, above</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O blessed, blessed night! I am afeard.<br>Being in night, all this is but a dream,<br>Too flattering-sweet to be substantial.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Re-enter JULIET, above</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Three words, dear Romeo, and good night indeed.<br>If that thy bent of love be honourable,<br>Thy purpose marriage, send me word to-morrow,<br>By one that I'll procure to come to thee,<br>Where and what time thou wilt perform the rite;<br>And all my fortunes at thy foot I'll lay<br>And follow thee my lord throughout the world.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[Within] Madam!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I come, anon.--But if thou mean'st not well,<br>I do beseech thee--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[Within] Madam!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By and by, I come:--<br>To cease thy suit, and leave me to my grief:<br>To-morrow will I send.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">So thrive my soul--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A thousand times good night!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit, above</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A thousand times the worse, to want thy light.<br>Love goes toward love, as schoolboys from<br>their books,<br>But love from love, toward school with heavy looks.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Retiring</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Re-enter JULIET, above</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Hist! Romeo, hist! O, for a falconer's voice,<br>To lure this tassel-gentle back again!<br>Bondage is hoarse, and may not speak aloud;<br>Else would I tear the cave where Echo lies,<br>And make her airy tongue more hoarse than mine,<br>With repetition of my Romeo's name.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">It is my soul that calls upon my name:<br>How silver-sweet sound lovers' tongues by night,<br>Like softest music to attending ears!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My dear?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">At what o'clock to-morrow<br>Shall I send to thee?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">At the hour of nine.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will not fail: 'tis twenty years till then.<br>I have forgot why I did call thee back.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Let me stand here till thou remember it.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I shall forget, to have thee still stand there,<br>Remembering how I love thy company.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And I'll still stay, to have thee still forget,<br>Forgetting any other home but this.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis almost morning; I would have thee gone:<br>And yet no further than a wanton's bird;<br>Who lets it hop a little from her hand,<br>Like a poor prisoner in his twisted gyves,<br>And with a silk thread plucks it back again,<br>So loving-jealous of his liberty.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I would I were thy bird.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Sweet, so would I:<br>Yet I should kill thee with much cherishing.<br>Good night, good night! parting is such<br>sweet sorrow,<br>That I shall say good night till it be morrow.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit above</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Sleep dwell upon thine eyes, peace in thy breast!<br>Would I were sleep and peace, so sweet to rest!<br>Hence will I to my ghostly father's cell,<br>His help to crave, and my dear hap to tell.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE III. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE, with a basket</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The grey-eyed morn smiles on the frowning night,<br>Chequering the eastern clouds with streaks of light,<br>And flecked darkness like a drunkard reels<br>From forth day's path and Titan's fiery wheels:<br>Now, ere the sun advance his burning eye,<br>The day to cheer and night's dank dew to dry,<br>I must up-fill this osier cage of ours<br>With baleful weeds and precious-juiced flowers.<br>The earth that's nature's mother is her tomb;<br>What is her burying grave that is her womb,<br>And from her womb children of divers kind<br>We sucking on her natural bosom find,<br>Many for many virtues excellent,<br>None but for some and yet all different.<br>O, mickle is the powerful grace that lies<br>In herbs, plants, stones, and their true qualities:<br>For nought so vile that on the earth doth live<br>But to the earth some special good doth give,<br>Nor aught so good but strain'd from that fair use<br>Revolts from true birth, stumbling on abuse:<br>Virtue itself turns vice, being misapplied;<br>And vice sometimes by action dignified.<br>Within the infant rind of this small flower<br>Poison hath residence and medicine power:<br>For this, being smelt, with that part cheers each part;<br>Being tasted, slays all senses with the heart.<br>Two such opposed kings encamp them still<br>In man as well as herbs, grace and rude will;<br>And where the worser is predominant,<br>Full soon the canker death eats up that plant.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good morrow, father.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Benedicite!<br>What early tongue so sweet saluteth me?<br>Young son, it argues a distemper'd head<br>So soon to bid good morrow to thy bed:<br>Care keeps his watch in every old man's eye,<br>And where care lodges, sleep will never lie;<br>But where unbruised youth with unstuff'd brain<br>Doth couch his limbs, there golden sleep doth reign:<br>Therefore thy earliness doth me assure<br>Thou art up-roused by some distemperature;<br>Or if not so, then here I hit it right,<br>Our Romeo hath not been in bed to-night.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">That last is true; the sweeter rest was mine.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">God pardon sin! wast thou with Rosaline?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">With Rosaline, my ghostly father? no;<br>I have forgot that name, and that name's woe.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">That's my good son: but where hast thou been, then?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I'll tell thee, ere thou ask it me again.<br>I have been feasting with mine enemy,<br>Where on a sudden one hath wounded me,<br>That's by me wounded: both our remedies<br>Within thy help and holy physic lies:<br>I bear no hatred, blessed man, for, lo,<br>My intercession likewise steads my foe.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Be plain, good son, and homely in thy drift;<br>Riddling confession finds but riddling shrift.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then plainly know my heart's dear love is set<br>On the fair daughter of rich Capulet:<br>As mine on hers, so hers is set on mine;<br>And all combined, save what thou must combine<br>By holy marriage: when and where and how<br>We met, we woo'd and made exchange of vow,<br>I'll tell thee as we pass; but this I pray,<br>That thou consent to marry us to-day.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Holy Saint Francis, what a change is here!<br>Is Rosaline, whom thou didst love so dear,<br>So soon forsaken? young men's love then lies<br>Not truly in their hearts, but in their eyes.<br>Jesu Maria, what a deal of brine<br>Hath wash'd thy sallow cheeks for Rosaline!<br>How much salt water thrown away in waste,<br>To season love, that of it doth not taste!<br>The sun not yet thy sighs from heaven clears,<br>Thy old groans ring yet in my ancient ears;<br>Lo, here upon thy cheek the stain doth sit<br>Of an old tear that is not wash'd off yet:<br>If e'er thou wast thyself and these woes thine,<br>Thou and these woes were all for Rosaline:<br>And art thou changed? pronounce this sentence then,<br>Women may fall, when there's no strength in men.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou chid'st me oft for loving Rosaline.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">For doting, not for loving, pupil mine.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And bad'st me bury love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Not in a grave,<br>To lay one in, another out to have.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I pray thee, chide not; she whom I love now<br>Doth grace for grace and love for love allow;<br>The other did not so.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, she knew well<br>Thy love did read by rote and could not spell.<br>But come, young waverer, come, go with me,<br>In one respect I'll thy assistant be;<br>For this alliance may so happy prove,<br>To turn your households' rancour to pure love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, let us hence; I stand on sudden haste.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Wisely and slow; they stumble that run fast.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE IV. A street.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter BENVOLIO and MERCUTIO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Where the devil should this Romeo be?<br>Came he not home to-night?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Not to his father's; I spoke with his man.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ah, that same pale hard-hearted wench, that Rosaline.<br>Torments him so, that he will sure run mad.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tybalt, the kinsman of old Capulet,<br>Hath sent a letter to his father's house.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A challenge, on my life.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo will answer it.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Any man that can write may answer a letter.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, he will answer the letter's master, how he<br>dares, being dared.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Alas poor Romeo! he is already dead; stabbed with a<br>white wench's black eye; shot through the ear with a<br>love-song; the very pin of his heart cleft with the<br>blind bow-boy's butt-shaft: and is he a man to<br>encounter Tybalt?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, what is Tybalt?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">More than prince of cats, I can tell you. O, he is<br>the courageous captain of compliments. He fights as<br>you sing prick-song, keeps time, distance, and<br>proportion; rests me his minim rest, one, two, and<br>the third in your bosom: the very butcher of a silk<br>button, a duellist, a duellist; a gentleman of the<br>very first house, of the first and second cause:<br>ah, the immortal passado! the punto reverso! the<br>hai!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The what?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The pox of such antic, lisping, affecting<br>fantasticoes; these new tuners of accents! 'By Jesu,<br>a very good blade! a very tall man! a very good<br>whore!' Why, is not this a lamentable thing,<br>grandsire, that we should be thus afflicted with<br>these strange flies, these fashion-mongers, these<br>perdona-mi's, who stand so much on the new form,<br>that they cannot at ease on the old bench? O, their<br>bones, their bones!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here comes Romeo, here comes Romeo.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Without his roe, like a dried herring: flesh, flesh,<br>how art thou fishified! Now is he for the numbers<br>that Petrarch flowed in: Laura to his lady was but a<br>kitchen-wench; marry, she had a better love to<br>be-rhyme her; Dido a dowdy; Cleopatra a gipsy;<br>Helen and Hero hildings and harlots; Thisbe a grey<br>eye or so, but not to the purpose. Signior<br>Romeo, bon jour! there's a French salutation<br>to your French slop. You gave us the counterfeit<br>fairly last night.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good morrow to you both. What counterfeit did I give you?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The ship, sir, the slip; can you not conceive?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Pardon, good Mercutio, my business was great; and in<br>such a case as mine a man may strain courtesy.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">That's as much as to say, such a case as yours<br>constrains a man to bow in the hams.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Meaning, to court'sy.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou hast most kindly hit it.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A most courteous exposition.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, I am the very pink of courtesy.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Pink for flower.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Right.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, then is my pump well flowered.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well said: follow me this jest now till thou hast<br>worn out thy pump, that when the single sole of it<br>is worn, the jest may remain after the wearing sole singular.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O single-soled jest, solely singular for the<br>singleness.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come between us, good Benvolio; my wits faint.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Switch and spurs, switch and spurs; or I'll cry a match.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, if thy wits run the wild-goose chase, I have<br>done, for thou hast more of the wild-goose in one of<br>thy wits than, I am sure, I have in my whole five:<br>was I with you there for the goose?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou wast never with me for any thing when thou wast<br>not there for the goose.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will bite thee by the ear for that jest.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, good goose, bite not.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thy wit is a very bitter sweeting; it is a most<br>sharp sauce.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And is it not well served in to a sweet goose?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O here's a wit of cheveril, that stretches from an<br>inch narrow to an ell broad!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I stretch it out for that word 'broad;' which added<br>to the goose, proves thee far and wide a broad goose.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why, is not this better now than groaning for love?<br>now art thou sociable, now art thou Romeo; now art<br>thou what thou art, by art as well as by nature:<br>for this drivelling love is like a great natural,<br>that runs lolling up and down to hide his bauble in a hole.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Stop there, stop there.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou desirest me to stop in my tale against the hair.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou wouldst else have made thy tale large.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, thou art deceived; I would have made it short:<br>for I was come to the whole depth of my tale; and<br>meant, indeed, to occupy the argument no longer.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here's goodly gear!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Nurse and PETER</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A sail, a sail!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Two, two; a shirt and a smock.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Peter!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Anon!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My fan, Peter.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good Peter, to hide her face; for her fan's the<br>fairer face.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">God ye good morrow, gentlemen.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">God ye good den, fair gentlewoman.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Is it good den?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'Tis no less, I tell you, for the bawdy hand of the<br>dial is now upon the prick of noon.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Out upon you! what a man are you!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">One, gentlewoman, that God hath made for himself to<br>mar.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By my troth, it is well said; 'for himself to mar,'<br>quoth a'? Gentlemen, can any of you tell me where I<br>may find the young Romeo?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I can tell you; but young Romeo will be older when<br>you have found him than he was when you sought him:<br>I am the youngest of that name, for fault of a worse.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">You say well.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Yea, is the worst well? very well took, i' faith;<br>wisely, wisely.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">if you be he, sir, I desire some confidence with<br>you.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">She will indite him to some supper.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A bawd, a bawd, a bawd! so ho!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What hast thou found?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No hare, sir; unless a hare, sir, in a lenten pie,<br>that is something stale and hoar ere it be spent.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Sings</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">An old hare hoar,<br>And an old hare hoar,<br>Is very good meat in lent<br>But a hare that is hoar<br>Is too much for a score,<br>When it hoars ere it be spent.<br>Romeo, will you come to your father's? we'll<br>to dinner, thither.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will follow you.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Farewell, ancient lady; farewell,<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Singing</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">'lady, lady, lady.'<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Marry, farewell! I pray you, sir, what saucy<br>merchant was this, that was so full of his ropery?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A gentleman, nurse, that loves to hear himself talk,<br>and will speak more in a minute than he will stand<br>to in a month.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">An a' speak any thing against me, I'll take him<br>down, an a' were lustier than he is, and twenty such<br>Jacks; and if I cannot, I'll find those that shall.<br>Scurvy knave! I am none of his flirt-gills; I am<br>none of his skains-mates. And thou must stand by<br>too, and suffer every knave to use me at his pleasure?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I saw no man use you a pleasure; if I had, my weapon<br>should quickly have been out, I warrant you: I dare<br>draw as soon as another man, if I see occasion in a<br>good quarrel, and the law on my side.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now, afore God, I am so vexed, that every part about<br>me quivers. Scurvy knave! Pray you, sir, a word:<br>and as I told you, my young lady bade me inquire you<br>out; what she bade me say, I will keep to myself:<br>but first let me tell ye, if ye should lead her into<br>a fool's paradise, as they say, it were a very gross<br>kind of behavior, as they say: for the gentlewoman<br>is young; and, therefore, if you should deal double<br>with her, truly it were an ill thing to be offered<br>to any gentlewoman, and very weak dealing.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nurse, commend me to thy lady and mistress. I<br>protest unto thee--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good heart, and, i' faith, I will tell her as much:<br>Lord, Lord, she will be a joyful woman.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What wilt thou tell her, nurse? thou dost not mark me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will tell her, sir, that you do protest; which, as<br>I take it, is a gentlemanlike offer.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Bid her devise<br>Some means to come to shrift this afternoon;<br>And there she shall at Friar Laurence' cell<br>Be shrived and married. Here is for thy pains.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No truly sir; not a penny.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go to; I say you shall.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This afternoon, sir? well, she shall be there.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And stay, good nurse, behind the abbey wall:<br>Within this hour my man shall be with thee<br>And bring thee cords made like a tackled stair;<br>Which to the high top-gallant of my joy<br>Must be my convoy in the secret night.<br>Farewell; be trusty, and I'll quit thy pains:<br>Farewell; commend me to thy mistress.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now God in heaven bless thee! Hark you, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What say'st thou, my dear nurse?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Is your man secret? Did you ne'er hear say,<br>Two may keep counsel, putting one away?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I warrant thee, my man's as true as steel.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>NURSE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, sir; my mistress is the sweetest lady--Lord,<br>Lord! when 'twas a little prating thing:--O, there<br>is a nobleman in town, one Paris, that would fain<br>lay knife aboard; but she, good soul, had as lief<br>see a toad, a very toad, as see him. I anger her<br>sometimes and tell her that Paris is the properer<br>man; but, I'll warrant you, when I say so, she looks<br>as pale as any clout in the versal world. Doth not<br>rosemary and Romeo begin both with a letter?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, nurse; what of that? both with an R.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ah. mocker! that's the dog's name; R is for<br>the--No; I know it begins with some other<br>letter:--and she hath the prettiest sententious of<br>it, of you and rosemary, that it would do you good<br>to hear it.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Commend me to thy lady.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, a thousand times.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit Romeo</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Peter!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Anon!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Peter, take my fan, and go before and apace.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE V. Capulet's orchard.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter JULIET</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The clock struck nine when I did send the nurse;<br>In half an hour she promised to return.<br>Perchance she cannot meet him: that's not so.<br>O, she is lame! love's heralds should be thoughts,<br>Which ten times faster glide than the sun's beams,<br>Driving back shadows over louring hills:<br>Therefore do nimble-pinion'd doves draw love,<br>And therefore hath the wind-swift Cupid wings.<br>Now is the sun upon the highmost hill<br>Of this day's journey, and from nine till twelve<br>Is three long hours, yet she is not come.<br>Had she affections and warm youthful blood,<br>She would be as swift in motion as a ball;<br>My words would bandy her to my sweet love,<br>And his to me:<br>But old folks, many feign as they were dead;<br>Unwieldy, slow, heavy and pale as lead.<br>O God, she comes!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Nurse and PETER</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O honey nurse, what news?<br>Hast thou met with him? Send thy man away.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Peter, stay at the gate.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit PETER</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now, good sweet nurse,--O Lord, why look'st thou sad?<br>Though news be sad, yet tell them merrily;<br>If good, thou shamest the music of sweet news<br>By playing it to me with so sour a face.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I am a-weary, give me leave awhile:<br>Fie, how my bones ache! what a jaunt have I had!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I would thou hadst my bones, and I thy news:<br>Nay, come, I pray thee, speak; good, good nurse, speak.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Jesu, what haste? can you not stay awhile?<br>Do you not see that I am out of breath?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">How art thou out of breath, when thou hast breath<br>To say to me that thou art out of breath?<br>The excuse that thou dost make in this delay<br>Is longer than the tale thou dost excuse.<br>Is thy news good, or bad? answer to that;<br>Say either, and I'll stay the circumstance:<br>Let me be satisfied, is't good or bad?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, you have made a simple choice; you know not<br>how to choose a man: Romeo! no, not he; though his<br>face be better than any man's, yet his leg excels<br>all men's; and for a hand, and a foot, and a body,<br>though they be not to be talked on, yet they are<br>past compare: he is not the flower of courtesy,<br>but, I'll warrant him, as gentle as a lamb. Go thy<br>ways, wench; serve God. What, have you dined at home?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No, no: but all this did I know before.<br>What says he of our marriage? what of that?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Lord, how my head aches! what a head have I!<br>It beats as it would fall in twenty pieces.<br>My back o' t' other side,--O, my back, my back!<br>Beshrew your heart for sending me about,<br>To catch my death with jaunting up and down!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I' faith, I am sorry that thou art not well.<br>Sweet, sweet, sweet nurse, tell me, what says my love?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Your love says, like an honest gentleman, and a<br>courteous, and a kind, and a handsome, and, I<br>warrant, a virtuous,--Where is your mother?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Where is my mother! why, she is within;<br>Where should she be? How oddly thou repliest!<br>'Your love says, like an honest gentleman,<br>Where is your mother?'<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O God's lady dear!<br>Are you so hot? marry, come up, I trow;<br>Is this the poultice for my aching bones?<br>Henceforward do your messages yourself.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here's such a coil! come, what says Romeo?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Have you got leave to go to shrift to-day?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I have.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then hie you hence to Friar Laurence' cell;<br>There stays a husband to make you a wife:<br>Now comes the wanton blood up in your cheeks,<br>They'll be in scarlet straight at any news.<br>Hie you to church; I must another way,<br>To fetch a ladder, by the which your love<br>Must climb a bird's nest soon when it is dark:<br>I am the drudge and toil in your delight,<br>But you shall bear the burden soon at night.<br>Go; I'll to dinner: hie you to the cell.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Hie to high fortune! Honest nurse, farewell.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE VI. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">So smile the heavens upon this holy act,<br>That after hours with sorrow chide us not!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Amen, amen! but come what sorrow can,<br>It cannot countervail the exchange of joy<br>That one short minute gives me in her sight:<br>Do thou but close our hands with holy words,<br>Then love-devouring death do what he dare;<br>It is enough I may but call her mine.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">These violent delights have violent ends<br>And in their triumph die, like fire and powder,<br>Which as they kiss consume: the sweetest honey<br>Is loathsome in his own deliciousness<br>And in the taste confounds the appetite:<br>Therefore love moderately; long love doth so;<br>Too swift arrives as tardy as too slow.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter JULIET</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here comes the lady: O, so light a foot<br>Will ne'er wear out the everlasting flint:<br>A lover may bestride the gossamer<br>That idles in the wanton summer air,<br>And yet not fall; so light is vanity.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good even to my ghostly confessor.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo shall thank thee, daughter, for us both.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">As much to him, else is his thanks too much.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ah, Juliet, if the measure of thy joy<br>Be heap'd like mine and that thy skill be more<br>To blazon it, then sweeten with thy breath<br>This neighbour air, and let rich music's tongue<br>Unfold the imagined happiness that both<br>Receive in either by this dear encounter.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Conceit, more rich in matter than in words,<br>Brags of his substance, not of ornament:<br>They are but beggars that can count their worth;<br>But my true love is grown to such excess<br>I cannot sum up sum of half my wealth.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come, come with me, and we will make short work;<br>For, by your leaves, you shall not stay alone<br>Till holy church incorporate two in one.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">ACT III</h3><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE I. A public place.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter MERCUTIO, BENVOLIO, Page, and Servants</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I pray thee, good Mercutio, let's retire:<br>The day is hot, the Capulets abroad,<br>And, if we meet, we shall not scape a brawl;<br>For now, these hot days, is the mad blood stirring.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou art like one of those fellows that when he<br>enters the confines of a tavern claps me his sword<br>upon the table and says 'God send me no need of<br>thee!' and by the operation of the second cup draws<br>it on the drawer, when indeed there is no need.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Am I like such a fellow?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come, come, thou art as hot a Jack in thy mood as<br>any in Italy, and as soon moved to be moody, and as<br>soon moody to be moved.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And what to?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Nay, an there were two such, we should have none<br>shortly, for one would kill the other. Thou! why,<br>thou wilt quarrel with a man that hath a hair more,<br>or a hair less, in his beard, than thou hast: thou<br>wilt quarrel with a man for cracking nuts, having no<br>other reason but because thou hast hazel eyes: what<br>eye but such an eye would spy out such a quarrel?<br>Thy head is as fun of quarrels as an egg is full of<br>meat, and yet thy head hath been beaten as addle as<br>an egg for quarrelling: thou hast quarrelled with a<br>man for coughing in the street, because he hath<br>wakened thy dog that hath lain asleep in the sun:<br>didst thou not fall out with a tailor for wearing<br>his new doublet before Easter? with another, for<br>tying his new shoes with old riband? and yet thou<br>wilt tutor me from quarrelling!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">An I were so apt to quarrel as thou art, any man<br>should buy the fee-simple of my life for an hour and a quarter.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The fee-simple! O simple!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By my head, here come the Capulets.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">By my heel, I care not.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter TYBALT and others</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Follow me close, for I will speak to them.<br>Gentlemen, good den: a word with one of you.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And but one word with one of us? couple it with<br>something; make it a word and a blow.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">You shall find me apt enough to that, sir, an you<br>will give me occasion.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Could you not take some occasion without giving?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Mercutio, thou consort'st with Romeo,--<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Consort! what, dost thou make us minstrels? an<br>thou make minstrels of us, look to hear nothing but<br>discords: here's my fiddlestick; here's that shall<br>make you dance. 'Zounds, consort!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">We talk here in the public haunt of men:<br>Either withdraw unto some private place,<br>And reason coldly of your grievances,<br>Or else depart; here all eyes gaze on us.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Men's eyes were made to look, and let them gaze;<br>I will not budge for no man's pleasure, I.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, peace be with you, sir: here comes my man.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But I'll be hanged, sir, if he wear your livery:<br>Marry, go before to field, he'll be your follower;<br>Your worship in that sense may call him 'man.'<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo, the hate I bear thee can afford<br>No better term than this,--thou art a villain.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tybalt, the reason that I have to love thee<br>Doth much excuse the appertaining rage<br>To such a greeting: villain am I none;<br>Therefore farewell; I see thou know'st me not.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Boy, this shall not excuse the injuries<br>That thou hast done me; therefore turn and draw.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I do protest, I never injured thee,<br>But love thee better than thou canst devise,<br>Till thou shalt know the reason of my love:<br>And so, good Capulet,--which name I tender<br>As dearly as my own,--be satisfied.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O calm, dishonourable, vile submission!<br>Alla stoccata carries it away.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Draws</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tybalt, you rat-catcher, will you walk?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What wouldst thou have with me?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Good king of cats, nothing but one of your nine<br>lives; that I mean to make bold withal, and as you<br>shall use me hereafter, drybeat the rest of the<br>eight. Will you pluck your sword out of his pitcher<br>by the ears? make haste, lest mine be about your<br>ears ere it be out.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I am for you.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Drawing</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Gentle Mercutio, put thy rapier up.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come, sir, your passado.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>They fight</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Draw, Benvolio; beat down their weapons.<br>Gentlemen, for shame, forbear this outrage!<br>Tybalt, Mercutio, the prince expressly hath<br>Forbidden bandying in Verona streets:<br>Hold, Tybalt! good Mercutio!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>TYBALT under ROMEO's arm stabs MERCUTIO, and flies with his followers</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I am hurt.<br>A plague o' both your houses! I am sped.<br>Is he gone, and hath nothing?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What, art thou hurt?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, ay, a scratch, a scratch; marry, 'tis enough.<br>Where is my page? Go, villain, fetch a surgeon.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit Page</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Courage, man; the hurt cannot be much.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No, 'tis not so deep as a well, nor so wide as a<br>church-door; but 'tis enough,'twill serve: ask for<br>me to-morrow, and you shall find me a grave man. I<br>am peppered, I warrant, for this world. A plague o'<br>both your houses! 'Zounds, a dog, a rat, a mouse, a<br>cat, to scratch a man to death! a braggart, a<br>rogue, a villain, that fights by the book of<br>arithmetic! Why the devil came you between us? I<br>was hurt under your arm.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I thought all for the best.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MERCUTIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Help me into some house, Benvolio,<br>Or I shall faint. A plague o' both your houses!<br>They have made worms' meat of me: I have it,<br>And soundly too: your houses!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This gentleman, the prince's near ally,<br>My very friend, hath got his mortal hurt<br>In my behalf; my reputation stain'd<br>With Tybalt's slander,--Tybalt, that an hour<br>Hath been my kinsman! O sweet Juliet,<br>Thy beauty hath made me effeminate<br>And in my temper soften'd valour's steel!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Re-enter BENVOLIO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O Romeo, Romeo, brave Mercutio's dead!<br>That gallant spirit hath aspired the clouds,<br>Which too untimely here did scorn the earth.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This day's black fate on more days doth depend;<br>This but begins the woe, others must end.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here comes the furious Tybalt back again.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Alive, in triumph! and Mercutio slain!<br>Away to heaven, respective lenity,<br>And fire-eyed fury be my conduct now!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Re-enter TYBALT</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now, Tybalt, take the villain back again,<br>That late thou gavest me; for Mercutio's soul<br>Is but a little way above our heads,<br>Staying for thine to keep him company:<br>Either thou, or I, or both, must go with him.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>TYBALT</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou, wretched boy, that didst consort him here,<br>Shalt with him hence.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This shall determine that.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>They fight; TYBALT falls</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo, away, be gone!<br>The citizens are up, and Tybalt slain.<br>Stand not amazed: the prince will doom thee death,<br>If thou art taken: hence, be gone, away!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, I am fortune's fool!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why dost thou stay?<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit ROMEO</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Citizens, &amp; c</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Citizen</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Which way ran he that kill'd Mercutio?<br>Tybalt, that murderer, which way ran he?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">There lies that Tybalt.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Citizen</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Up, sir, go with me;<br>I charge thee in the princes name, obey.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Prince, attended; MONTAGUE, CAPULET, their Wives, and others</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Where are the vile beginners of this fray?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O noble prince, I can discover all<br>The unlucky manage of this fatal brawl:<br>There lies the man, slain by young Romeo,<br>That slew thy kinsman, brave Mercutio.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tybalt, my cousin! O my brother's child!<br>O prince! O cousin! husband! O, the blood is spilt<br>O my dear kinsman! Prince, as thou art true,<br>For blood of ours, shed blood of Montague.<br>O cousin, cousin!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Benvolio, who began this bloody fray?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BENVOLIO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tybalt, here slain, whom Romeo's hand did slay;<br>Romeo that spoke him fair, bade him bethink<br>How nice the quarrel was, and urged withal<br>Your high displeasure: all this uttered<br>With gentle breath, calm look, knees humbly bow'd,<br>Could not take truce with the unruly spleen<br>Of Tybalt deaf to peace, but that he tilts<br>With piercing steel at bold Mercutio's breast,<br>Who all as hot, turns deadly point to point,<br>And, with a martial scorn, with one hand beats<br>Cold death aside, and with the other sends<br>It back to Tybalt, whose dexterity,<br>Retorts it: Romeo he cries aloud,<br>'Hold, friends! friends, part!' and, swifter than<br>his tongue,<br>His agile arm beats down their fatal points,<br>And 'twixt them rushes; underneath whose arm<br>An envious thrust from Tybalt hit the life<br>Of stout Mercutio, and then Tybalt fled;<br>But by and by comes back to Romeo,<br>Who had but newly entertain'd revenge,<br>And to 't they go like lightning, for, ere I<br>Could draw to part them, was stout Tybalt slain.<br>And, as he fell, did Romeo turn and fly.<br>This is the truth, or let Benvolio die.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">He is a kinsman to the Montague;<br>Affection makes him false; he speaks not true:<br>Some twenty of them fought in this black strife,<br>And all those twenty could but kill one life.<br>I beg for justice, which thou, prince, must give;<br>Romeo slew Tybalt, Romeo must not live.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo slew him, he slew Mercutio;<br>Who now the price of his dear blood doth owe?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Not Romeo, prince, he was Mercutio's friend;<br>His fault concludes but what the law should end,<br>The life of Tybalt.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">And for that offence<br>Immediately we do exile him hence:<br>I have an interest in your hate's proceeding,<br>My blood for your rude brawls doth lie a-bleeding;<br>But I'll amerce you with so strong a fine<br>That you shall all repent the loss of mine:<br>I will be deaf to pleading and excuses;<br>Nor tears nor prayers shall purchase out abuses:<br>Therefore use none: let Romeo hence in haste,<br>Else, when he's found, that hour is his last.<br>Bear hence this body and attend our will:<br>Mercy but murders, pardoning those that kill.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE II. Capulet's orchard.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter JULIET</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Gallop apace, you fiery-footed steeds,<br>Towards Phoebus' lodging: such a wagoner<br>As Phaethon would whip you to the west,<br>And bring in cloudy night immediately.<br>Spread thy close curtain, love-performing night,<br>That runaway's eyes may wink and Romeo<br>Leap to these arms, untalk'd of and unseen.<br>Lovers can see to do their amorous rites<br>By their own beauties; or, if love be blind,<br>It best agrees with night. Come, civil night,<br>Thou sober-suited matron, all in black,<br>And learn me how to lose a winning match,<br>Play'd for a pair of stainless maidenhoods:<br>Hood my unmann'd blood, bating in my cheeks,<br>With thy black mantle; till strange love, grown bold,<br>Think true love acted simple modesty.<br>Come, night; come, Romeo; come, thou day in night;<br>For thou wilt lie upon the wings of night<br>Whiter than new snow on a raven's back.<br>Come, gentle night, come, loving, black-brow'd night,<br>Give me my Romeo; and, when he shall die,<br>Take him and cut him out in little stars,<br>And he will make the face of heaven so fine<br>That all the world will be in love with night<br>And pay no worship to the garish sun.<br>O, I have bought the mansion of a love,<br>But not possess'd it, and, though I am sold,<br>Not yet enjoy'd: so tedious is this day<br>As is the night before some festival<br>To an impatient child that hath new robes<br>And may not wear them. O, here comes my nurse,<br>And she brings news; and every tongue that speaks<br>But Romeo's name speaks heavenly eloquence.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Nurse, with cords</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now, nurse, what news? What hast thou there? the cords<br>That Romeo bid thee fetch?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, ay, the cords.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Throws them down</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay me! what news? why dost thou wring thy hands?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ah, well-a-day! he's dead, he's dead, he's dead!<br>We are undone, lady, we are undone!<br>Alack the day! he's gone, he's kill'd, he's dead!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Can heaven be so envious?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo can,<br>Though heaven cannot: O Romeo, Romeo!<br>Who ever would have thought it?
