and, madam, go with him;<br>And go, Sir Paris; every one prepare<br>To follow this fair corse unto her grave:<br>The heavens do lour upon you for some ill;<br>Move them no more by crossing their high will.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, PARIS, and FRIAR LAURENCE</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Faith, we may put up our pipes, and be gone.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Nurse</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Honest goodfellows, ah, put up, put up;<br>For, well you know, this is a pitiful case.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Ay, by my troth, the case may be amended.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter PETER</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Musicians, O, musicians, 'Heart's ease, Heart's<br>ease:' O, an you will have me live, play 'Heart's ease.'<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Why 'Heart's ease?'<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, musicians, because my heart itself plays 'My<br>heart is full of woe:' O, play me some merry dump,<br>to comfort me.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Not a dump we; 'tis no time to play now.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">You will not, then?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will then give it you soundly.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What will you give us?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No money, on my faith, but the gleek;<br>I will give you the minstrel.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then I will give you the serving-creature.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then will I lay the serving-creature's dagger on<br>your pate. I will carry no crotchets: I'll re you,<br>I'll fa you; do you note me?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">An you re us and fa us, you note us.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Pray you, put up your dagger, and put out your wit.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then have at you with my wit! I will dry-beat you<br>with an iron wit, and put up my iron dagger. Answer<br>me like men:<br>'When griping grief the heart doth wound,<br>And doleful dumps the mind oppress,<br>Then music with her silver sound'--<br>why 'silver sound'? why 'music with her silver<br>sound'? What say you, Simon Catling?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Marry, sir, because silver hath a sweet sound.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Pretty! What say you, Hugh Rebeck?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I say 'silver sound,' because musicians sound for silver.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Pretty too! What say you, James Soundpost?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Third Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Faith, I know not what to say.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PETER</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, I cry you mercy; you are the singer: I will say<br>for you. It is 'music with her silver sound,'<br>because musicians have no gold for sounding:<br>'Then music with her silver sound<br>With speedy help doth lend redress.'<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What a pestilent knave is this same!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Musician</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Hang him, Jack! Come, we'll in here; tarry for the<br>mourners, and stay dinner.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">ACT V</h3><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE I. Mantua. A street.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">If I may trust the flattering truth of sleep,<br>My dreams presage some joyful news at hand:<br>My bosom's lord sits lightly in his throne;<br>And all this day an unaccustom'd spirit<br>Lifts me above the ground with cheerful thoughts.<br>I dreamt my lady came and found me dead--<br>Strange dream, that gives a dead man leave<br>to think!--<br>And breathed such life with kisses in my lips,<br>That I revived, and was an emperor.<br>Ah me! how sweet is love itself possess'd,<br>When but love's shadows are so rich in joy!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter BALTHASAR, booted</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">News from Verona!--How now, Balthasar!<br>Dost thou not bring me letters from the friar?<br>How doth my lady? Is my father well?<br>How fares my Juliet? that I ask again;<br>For nothing can be ill, if she be well.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then she is well, and nothing can be ill:<br>Her body sleeps in Capel's monument,<br>And her immortal part with angels lives.<br>I saw her laid low in her kindred's vault,<br>And presently took post to tell it you:<br>O, pardon me for bringing these ill news,<br>Since you did leave it for my office, sir.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Is it even so? then I defy you, stars!<br>Thou know'st my lodging: get me ink and paper,<br>And hire post-horses; I will hence to-night.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I do beseech you, sir, have patience:<br>Your looks are pale and wild, and do import<br>Some misadventure.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Tush, thou art deceived:<br>Leave me, and do the thing I bid thee do.<br>Hast thou no letters to me from the friar?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No, my good lord.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">No matter: get thee gone,<br>And hire those horses; I'll be with thee straight.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit BALTHASAR</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Well, Juliet, I will lie with thee to-night.<br>Let's see for means: O mischief, thou art swift<br>To enter in the thoughts of desperate men!<br>I do remember an apothecary,--<br>And hereabouts he dwells,--which late I noted<br>In tatter'd weeds, with overwhelming brows,<br>Culling of simples; meagre were his looks,<br>Sharp misery had worn him to the bones:<br>And in his needy shop a tortoise hung,<br>An alligator stuff'd, and other skins<br>Of ill-shaped fishes; and about his shelves<br>A beggarly account of empty boxes,<br>Green earthen pots, bladders and musty seeds,<br>Remnants of packthread and old cakes of roses,<br>Were thinly scatter'd, to make up a show.<br>Noting this penury, to myself I said<br>'An if a man did need a poison now,<br>Whose sale is present death in Mantua,<br>Here lives a caitiff wretch would sell it him.'<br>O, this same thought did but forerun my need;<br>And this same needy man must sell it me.<br>As I remember, this should be the house.<br>Being holiday, the beggar's shop is shut.<br>What, ho! apothecary!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Apothecary</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Apothecary</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Who calls so loud?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come hither, man. I see that thou art poor:<br>Hold, there is forty ducats: let me have<br>A dram of poison, such soon-speeding gear<br>As will disperse itself through all the veins<br>That the life-weary taker may fall dead<br>And that the trunk may be discharged of breath<br>As violently as hasty powder fired<br>Doth hurry from the fatal cannon's womb.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Apothecary</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Such mortal drugs I have; but Mantua's law<br>Is death to any he that utters them.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Art thou so bare and full of wretchedness,<br>And fear'st to die? famine is in thy cheeks,<br>Need and oppression starveth in thine eyes,<br>Contempt and beggary hangs upon thy back;<br>The world is not thy friend nor the world's law;<br>The world affords no law to make thee rich;<br>Then be not poor, but break it, and take this.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Apothecary</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">My poverty, but not my will, consents.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I pay thy poverty, and not thy will.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Apothecary</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Put this in any liquid thing you will,<br>And drink it off; and, if you had the strength<br>Of twenty men, it would dispatch you straight.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">There is thy gold, worse poison to men's souls,<br>Doing more murders in this loathsome world,<br>Than these poor compounds that thou mayst not sell.<br>I sell thee poison; thou hast sold me none.<br>Farewell: buy food, and get thyself in flesh.<br>Come, cordial and not poison, go with me<br>To Juliet's grave; for there must I use thee.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE II. Friar Laurence's cell.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter FRIAR JOHN</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR JOHN</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Holy Franciscan friar! brother, ho!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter FRIAR LAURENCE</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This same should be the voice of Friar John.<br>Welcome from Mantua: what says Romeo?<br>Or, if his mind be writ, give me his letter.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR JOHN</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Going to find a bare-foot brother out<br>One of our order, to associate me,<br>Here in this city visiting the sick,<br>And finding him, the searchers of the town,<br>Suspecting that we both were in a house<br>Where the infectious pestilence did reign,<br>Seal'd up the doors, and would not let us forth;<br>So that my speed to Mantua there was stay'd.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Who bare my letter, then, to Romeo?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR JOHN</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I could not send it,--here it is again,--<br>Nor get a messenger to bring it thee,<br>So fearful were they of infection.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Unhappy fortune! by my brotherhood,<br>The letter was not nice but full of charge<br>Of dear import, and the neglecting it<br>May do much danger. Friar John, go hence;<br>Get me an iron crow, and bring it straight<br>Unto my cell.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR JOHN</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Brother, I'll go and bring it thee.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Now must I to the monument alone;<br>Within three hours will fair Juliet wake:<br>She will beshrew me much that Romeo<br>Hath had no notice of these accidents;<br>But I will write again to Mantua,<br>And keep her at my cell till Romeo come;<br>Poor living corse, closed in a dead man's tomb!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><h3 style="white-space:pre-wrap;">SCENE III. A churchyard; in it a tomb belonging to the Capulets.</h3><p class="" data-rte-preserve-empty="true" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter PARIS, and his Page bearing flowers and a torch</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PARIS</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Give me thy torch, boy: hence, and stand aloof:<br>Yet put it out, for I would not be seen.<br>Under yond yew-trees lay thee all along,<br>Holding thine ear close to the hollow ground;<br>So shall no foot upon the churchyard tread,<br>Being loose, unfirm, with digging up of graves,<br>But thou shalt hear it: whistle then to me,<br>As signal that thou hear'st something approach.<br>Give me those flowers. Do as I bid thee, go.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PAGE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[Aside] I am almost afraid to stand alone<br>Here in the churchyard; yet I will adventure.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Retires</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PARIS</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Sweet flower, with flowers thy bridal bed I strew,--<br>O woe! thy canopy is dust and stones;--<br>Which with sweet water nightly I will dew,<br>Or, wanting that, with tears distill'd by moans:<br>The obsequies that I for thee will keep<br>Nightly shall be to strew thy grave and weep.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>The Page whistles</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The boy gives warning something doth approach.<br>What cursed foot wanders this way to-night,<br>To cross my obsequies and true love's rite?<br>What with a torch! muffle me, night, awhile.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Retires</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter ROMEO and BALTHASAR, with a torch, mattock, &amp; c</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Give me that mattock and the wrenching iron.<br>Hold, take this letter; early in the morning<br>See thou deliver it to my lord and father.<br>Give me the light: upon thy life, I charge thee,<br>Whate'er thou hear'st or seest, stand all aloof,<br>And do not interrupt me in my course.<br>Why I descend into this bed of death,<br>Is partly to behold my lady's face;<br>But chiefly to take thence from her dead finger<br>A precious ring, a ring that I must use<br>In dear employment: therefore hence, be gone:<br>But if thou, jealous, dost return to pry<br>In what I further shall intend to do,<br>By heaven, I will tear thee joint by joint<br>And strew this hungry churchyard with thy limbs:<br>The time and my intents are savage-wild,<br>More fierce and more inexorable far<br>Than empty tigers or the roaring sea.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will be gone, sir, and not trouble you.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">So shalt thou show me friendship. Take thou that:<br>Live, and be prosperous: and farewell, good fellow.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[Aside] For all this same, I'll hide me hereabout:<br>His looks I fear, and his intents I doubt.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Retires</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thou detestable maw, thou womb of death,<br>Gorged with the dearest morsel of the earth,<br>Thus I enforce thy rotten jaws to open,<br>And, in despite, I'll cram thee with more food!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Opens the tomb</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PARIS</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This is that banish'd haughty Montague,<br>That murder'd my love's cousin, with which grief,<br>It is supposed, the fair creature died;<br>And here is come to do some villanous shame<br>To the dead bodies: I will apprehend him.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Comes forward</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Stop thy unhallow'd toil, vile Montague!<br>Can vengeance be pursued further than death?<br>Condemned villain, I do apprehend thee:<br>Obey, and go with me; for thou must die.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I must indeed; and therefore came I hither.<br>Good gentle youth, tempt not a desperate man;<br>Fly hence, and leave me: think upon these gone;<br>Let them affright thee. I beseech thee, youth,<br>Put not another sin upon my head,<br>By urging me to fury: O, be gone!<br>By heaven, I love thee better than myself;<br>For I come hither arm'd against myself:<br>Stay not, be gone; live, and hereafter say,<br>A madman's mercy bade thee run away.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PARIS</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I do defy thy conjurations,<br>And apprehend thee for a felon here.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Wilt thou provoke me? then have at thee, boy!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>They fight</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PAGE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O Lord, they fight! I will go call the watch.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PARIS</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O, I am slain!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Falls</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">If thou be merciful,<br>Open the tomb, lay me with Juliet.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Dies</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>ROMEO</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">In faith, I will. Let me peruse this face.<br>Mercutio's kinsman, noble County Paris!<br>What said my man, when my betossed soul<br>Did not attend him as we rode? I think<br>He told me Paris should have married Juliet:<br>Said he not so? or did I dream it so?<br>Or am I mad, hearing him talk of Juliet,<br>To think it was so? O, give me thy hand,<br>One writ with me in sour misfortune's book!<br>I'll bury thee in a triumphant grave;<br>A grave? O no! a lantern, slaughter'd youth,<br>For here lies Juliet, and her beauty makes<br>This vault a feasting presence full of light.<br>Death, lie thou there, by a dead man interr'd.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Laying PARIS in the tomb</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">How oft when men are at the point of death<br>Have they been merry! which their keepers call<br>A lightning before death: O, how may I<br>Call this a lightning? O my love! my wife!<br>Death, that hath suck'd the honey of thy breath,<br>Hath had no power yet upon thy beauty:<br>Thou art not conquer'd; beauty's ensign yet<br>Is crimson in thy lips and in thy cheeks,<br>And death's pale flag is not advanced there.<br>Tybalt, liest thou there in thy bloody sheet?<br>O, what more favour can I do to thee,<br>Than with that hand that cut thy youth in twain<br>To sunder his that was thine enemy?<br>Forgive me, cousin! Ah, dear Juliet,<br>Why art thou yet so fair? shall I believe<br>That unsubstantial death is amorous,<br>And that the lean abhorred monster keeps<br>Thee here in dark to be his paramour?<br>For fear of that, I still will stay with thee;<br>And never from this palace of dim night<br>Depart again: here, here will I remain<br>With worms that are thy chamber-maids; O, here<br>Will I set up my everlasting rest,<br>And shake the yoke of inauspicious stars<br>From this world-wearied flesh. Eyes, look your last!<br>Arms, take your last embrace! and, lips, O you<br>The doors of breath, seal with a righteous kiss<br>A dateless bargain to engrossing death!<br>Come, bitter conduct, come, unsavoury guide!<br>Thou desperate pilot, now at once run on<br>The dashing rocks thy sea-sick weary bark!<br>Here's to my love!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Drinks</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O true apothecary!<br>Thy drugs are quick. Thus with a kiss I die.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Dies</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter, at the other end of the churchyard, FRIAR LAURENCE, with a lantern, crow, and spade</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Saint Francis be my speed! how oft to-night<br>Have my old feet stumbled at graves! Who's there?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here's one, a friend, and one that knows you well.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Bliss be upon you! Tell me, good my friend,<br>What torch is yond, that vainly lends his light<br>To grubs and eyeless skulls? as I discern,<br>It burneth in the Capel's monument.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">It doth so, holy sir; and there's my master,<br>One that you love.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Who is it?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">How long hath he been there?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Full half an hour.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go with me to the vault.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I dare not, sir<br>My master knows not but I am gone hence;<br>And fearfully did menace me with death,<br>If I did stay to look on his intents.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Stay, then; I'll go alone. Fear comes upon me:<br>O, much I fear some ill unlucky thing.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">As I did sleep under this yew-tree here,<br>I dreamt my master and another fought,<br>And that my master slew him.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Advances</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Alack, alack, what blood is this, which stains<br>The stony entrance of this sepulchre?<br>What mean these masterless and gory swords<br>To lie discolour'd by this place of peace?<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enters the tomb</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Romeo! O, pale! Who else? what, Paris too?<br>And steep'd in blood? Ah, what an unkind hour<br>Is guilty of this lamentable chance!<br>The lady stirs.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>JULIET wakes</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O comfortable friar! where is my lord?<br>I do remember well where I should be,<br>And there I am. Where is my Romeo?<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Noise within</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I hear some noise. Lady, come from that nest<br>Of death, contagion, and unnatural sleep:<br>A greater power than we can contradict<br>Hath thwarted our intents. Come, come away.<br>Thy husband in thy bosom there lies dead;<br>And Paris too. Come, I'll dispose of thee<br>Among a sisterhood of holy nuns:<br>Stay not to question, for the watch is coming;<br>Come, go, good Juliet,<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Noise again</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I dare no longer stay.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Go, get thee hence, for I will not away.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exit FRIAR LAURENCE</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What's here? a cup, closed in my true love's hand?<br>Poison, I see, hath been his timeless end:<br>O churl! drunk all, and left no friendly drop<br>To help me after? I will kiss thy lips;<br>Haply some poison yet doth hang on them,<br>To make die with a restorative.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Kisses him</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Thy lips are warm.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">[Within] Lead, boy: which way?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>JULIET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Yea, noise? then I'll be brief. O happy dagger!<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Snatching ROMEO's dagger</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This is thy sheath;<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Stabs herself</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">there rust, and let me die.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Falls on ROMEO's body, and dies</em></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter Watch, with the Page of PARIS</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PAGE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This is the place; there, where the torch doth burn.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The ground is bloody; search about the churchyard:<br>Go, some of you, whoe'er you find attach.<br>Pitiful sight! here lies the county slain,<br>And Juliet bleeding, warm, and newly dead,<br>Who here hath lain these two days buried.<br>Go, tell the prince: run to the Capulets:<br>Raise up the Montagues: some others search:<br>We see the ground whereon these woes do lie;<br>But the true ground of all these piteous woes<br>We cannot without circumstance descry.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Re-enter some of the Watch, with BALTHASAR</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Second Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here's Romeo's man; we found him in the churchyard.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Hold him in safety, till the prince come hither.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Re-enter others of the Watch, with FRIAR LAURENCE</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>Third Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here is a friar, that trembles, sighs and weeps:<br>We took this mattock and this spade from him,<br>As he was coming from this churchyard side.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A great suspicion: stay the friar too.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter the PRINCE and Attendants</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What misadventure is so early up,<br>That calls our person from our morning's rest?<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, and others</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What should it be, that they so shriek abroad?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">The people in the street cry Romeo,<br>Some Juliet, and some Paris; and all run,<br>With open outcry toward our monument.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">What fear is this which startles in our ears?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Sovereign, here lies the County Paris slain;<br>And Romeo dead; and Juliet, dead before,<br>Warm and new kill'd.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Search, seek, and know how this foul murder comes.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>First Watchman</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Here is a friar, and slaughter'd Romeo's man;<br>With instruments upon them, fit to open<br>These dead men's tombs.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O heavens! O wife, look how our daughter bleeds!<br>This dagger hath mista'en--for, lo, his house<br>Is empty on the back of Montague,--<br>And it mis-sheathed in my daughter's bosom!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>LADY CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O me! this sight of death is as a bell,<br>That warns my old age to a sepulchre.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Enter MONTAGUE and others</em></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Come, Montague; for thou art early up,<br>To see thy son and heir more early down.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Alas, my liege, my wife is dead to-night;<br>Grief of my son's exile hath stopp'd her breath:<br>What further woe conspires against mine age?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Look, and thou shalt see.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O thou untaught! what manners is in this?<br>To press before thy father to a grave?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Seal up the mouth of outrage for a while,<br>Till we can clear these ambiguities,<br>And know their spring, their head, their<br>true descent;<br>And then will I be general of your woes,<br>And lead you even to death: meantime forbear,<br>And let mischance be slave to patience.<br>Bring forth the parties of suspicion.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I am the greatest, able to do least,<br>Yet most suspected, as the time and place<br>Doth make against me of this direful murder;<br>And here I stand, both to impeach and purge<br>Myself condemned and myself excused.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Then say at once what thou dost know in this.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>FRIAR LAURENCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I will be brief, for my short date of breath<br>Is not so long as is a tedious tale.<br>Romeo, there dead, was husband to that Juliet;<br>And she, there dead, that Romeo's faithful wife:<br>I married them; and their stol'n marriage-day<br>Was Tybalt's dooms-day, whose untimely death<br>Banish'd the new-made bridegroom from the city,<br>For whom, and not for Tybalt, Juliet pined.<br>You, to remove that siege of grief from her,<br>Betroth'd and would have married her perforce<br>To County Paris: then comes she to me,<br>And, with wild looks, bid me devise some mean<br>To rid her from this second marriage,<br>Or in my cell there would she kill herself.<br>Then gave I her, so tutor'd by my art,<br>A sleeping potion; which so took effect<br>As I intended, for it wrought on her<br>The form of death: meantime I writ to Romeo,<br>That he should hither come as this dire night,<br>To help to take her from her borrow'd grave,<br>Being the time the potion's force should cease.<br>But he which bore my letter, Friar John,<br>Was stay'd by accident, and yesternight<br>Return'd my letter back. Then all alone<br>At the prefixed hour of her waking,<br>Came I to take her from her kindred's vault;<br>Meaning to keep her closely at my cell,<br>Till I conveniently could send to Romeo:<br>But when I came, some minute ere the time<br>Of her awaking, here untimely lay<br>The noble Paris and true Romeo dead.<br>She wakes; and I entreated her come forth,<br>And bear this work of heaven with patience:<br>But then a noise did scare me from the tomb;<br>And she, too desperate, would not go with me,<br>But, as it seems, did violence on herself.<br>All this I know; and to the marriage<br>Her nurse is privy: and, if aught in this<br>Miscarried by my fault, let my old life<br>Be sacrificed, some hour before his time,<br>Unto the rigour of severest law.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">We still have known thee for a holy man.<br>Where's Romeo's man? what can he say in this?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>BALTHASAR</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">I brought my master news of Juliet's death;<br>And then in post he came from Mantua<br>To this same place, to this same monument.<br>This letter he early bid me give his father,<br>And threatened me with death, going in the vault,<br>I departed not and left him there.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">Give me the letter; I will look on it.<br>Where is the county's page, that raised the watch?<br>Sirrah, what made your master in this place?<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PAGE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">He came with flowers to strew his lady's grave;<br>And bid me stand aloof, and so I did:<br>Anon comes one with light to ope the tomb;<br>And by and by my master drew on him;<br>And then I ran away to call the watch.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">This letter doth make good the friar's words,<br>Their course of love, the tidings of her death:<br>And here he writes that he did buy a poison<br>Of a poor 'pothecary, and therewithal<br>Came to this vault to die, and lie with Juliet.<br>Where be these enemies? Capulet! Montague!<br>See, what a scourge is laid upon your hate,<br>That heaven finds means to kill your joys with love.<br>And I for winking at your discords too<br>Have lost a brace of kinsmen: all are punish'd.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">O brother Montague, give me thy hand:<br>This is my daughter's jointure, for no more<br>Can I demand.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>MONTAGUE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">But I can give thee more:<br>For I will raise her statue in pure gold;<br>That while Verona by that name is known,<br>There shall no figure at such rate be set<br>As that of true and faithful Juliet.<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>CAPULET</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">As rich shall Romeo's by his lady's lie;<br>Poor sacrifices of our enmity!<br></p></blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><strong>PRINCE</strong></p><blockquote><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;">A glooming peace this morning with it brings;<br>The sun, for sorrow, will not show his head:<br>Go hence, to have more talk of these sad things;<br>Some shall be pardon'd, and some punished:<br>For never was a story of more woe<br>Than this of Juliet and her Romeo.<br></p><p class="" style="white-space:pre-wrap;"><em>Exeunt</em></p></blockquote>
</div>




















  
  



</div></div></div></div></div></div>
